<div id="review_364610492" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/364610492" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9561928-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1337170792p2/9561928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/364610492?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/9561928-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781845769086" href="/book/show/3340429-wanted">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=setting-2000s">setting-2000s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=comic-graphic-novel">comic-graphic-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=african-american-major-character">african-american-major-character</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=gay">gay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=gay-major-character">gay-major-character</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=racism">racism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9561928-elizabeth?shelf=rape">rape</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer364610492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17892104996530557822">Okay, this is not Punisher Max. If you read the review of Punisher Max there are lots of passage you can just copy and paste in for Wanted - the stuff about strength being represented as the ability to rape, as being as racist as you please, as exalting in everything from genital mutilation to cannibalism. But this is not Punisher Max. This has a point. There actually is a point to Wanted. And that's the best thing that can be said for it.<br/><br/>Wanted is about what if the bad guys won? What is the wor</span>
  <span id="freeText17892104996530557822" style="display:none">Okay, this is not Punisher Max. If you read the review of Punisher Max there are lots of passage you can just copy and paste in for Wanted - the stuff about strength being represented as the ability to rape, as being as racist as you please, as exalting in everything from genital mutilation to cannibalism. But this is not Punisher Max. This has a point. There actually is a point to Wanted. And that's the best thing that can be said for it.<br /><br />Wanted is about what if the bad guys won? What is the worst thing the super-villains of the world could actually do? And it gives the answer: simple, just make the world as boring and dull as it actually is. The plot to Wanted is that, during the 80s, the super-villains won. They won and their terrible victory was to not only to beat the superheroes, but to make it so everyone thought they were just made-up stories, faintly ridiculous characters from tv or comics. It killed hope and it made everyone content simply to be ground under the machinery of the evil elite who just raked in money and did what they liked. In its way, it actually could have been a very interesting look at class and accepted villainy within real life. <br /><br />Needless to say it's not. It's the hook on which Millar hangs one long racist, misogynistic, homophobic orgy of violence. I've already said a lot of these kind of criticisms recently, and I don't feel like repeating myself so I'll just give you a choice sampling. <br /><br />The main character has to work for an AFRICAN-AMERICAN BOSS. Those words are continuously repeated in bold type: AFRICAN-AMERICAN BOSS. So you know, he has to work for a nigger, I mean that's the level of degradation he has submit to. And she's a woman as well - the indignity! The "fat lesbian"! Gee, I wonder why this guy is stuck in a dead-end job when he's such a charmer.<br /><br />It's not just women in the workplace he has a problem with, its his girlfriend, its his single mother for raising "to be such a fucking pussy", its the girl who turned him down for the prom that he strangles, its the women he rapes, the women he randomly shoots in the street. Well, he just hates women generally.<br /><br />As for the racism, its constant too, from the “Cholo fucks” to “You know why Spics smell so bad? . . . So blind people can hate ‘em too”. And this is just from the main character, the protagonist, you know, the one you’re supposed to identify with, and give a fuck about what happens to him.<br /><br />I’ll tell you what this reads like. This reads like someone who watched Fight Club and doesn’t get it. It has that “look at you, looking at me, you pathetic fuck” attitude which you get from other works where the protagonist is dissatisfied with a normal life, from everything from Fight Club to Trainspotting. But firstly, unlike Wanted, it’s not a direct “fuck you” to the reader, it’s a dissatisfaction with what we’ve all come to accept as a normal life. Secondly, the main character in Wanted doesn’t want to opt-out of, or destroy what has become normal life - he simply wants to succeed, succeed financially, succeed with women, succeed in every base sphere of desire, to the absolute detriment of any and everyone around him. Thirdly, there is no character arc in Wanted, he doesn’t decide to get clean, or to topple the system that keeps him down. He simply reaches the climax of the story, goes, “Well, that was great. It’s so much fun being an asshole” and walks off into the sunset.<br /><br />I say it reads like someone who watched Fight Club and doesn’t get it because it reads like what Fight Club would be if it was written by someone with no intelligence. The conversation between the main character and Tyler in Fight Club where they discuss how much they feel the want of a concrete notion of masculinity in the modern world, as a generation of men raised by women (in the abandonment by their biological fathers) - it’s like someone read that and went “Aye, women are bitches, take back your dicks!” and then went on to write a story about a man who hates his mother and rapes women to feel like a man. Again, Wanted has the maturity level of the type of fourteen-year-old that thinks Marilyn Manson’s a legend for eating his own turd on stage (Note: this is not a statement of criticism of Marilyn Manson as a band or anyone who likes them. It is a statement of criticism against anyone who thinks eating your own shit is impressive.) For me what stands as a benchmark of the intellectual dwarfism in this book, is the scene with the Professor in the limo. The Professor’s meant to be “Level 9” intelligence, basically the greatest genius on the planet. So how does Mark Millar show this to the lovely people in the audience? By having him complete a number of Rubik's cubes in the back of the limo. Because, wow, that’s smart. *sigh* I almost don’t feel like I need to write anything here but I’ll just leave it at this: if you’re idea of some really, REALLY smart, is someone who can do a Rubik’s cube really fast - you’re an idiot.<br /><br />Okay, so where are we? Racist - check. Misogynistic - check. Dumb as fuck - check. Oh, now we can actually get onto the writing. Yeah, the story, remember that? It’s shite. Look, I’m gonna say this one more time, you cannot be ‘realistic’, ‘gritty’ and ‘dark’ if you cannot write anything faintly resembling reality. If you write one-dimensional bilge, that’s what you’re gonna have at the end of the day. Everyone’s an asshole - it’s so real! No it’s not. People, real people, have more than one aspect to their personalities. And an actually surprising amount of the time, that aspect isn’t being as unpleasant as humanly possible. People actually relate to other people in a bunch of different ways. It’s called the vast diversity of human existence. <br /><br />Skip to the end. The climax (SPOILERS!) has the main character shoot his father in the head, at the man’s own insistence, in what is an inexplicably tender scene for two people who’ve met each other only a few hours ago. The main character then delivers a speech denouncing this way of living, unable to have a normal family life with no chance of retirement, and announcing he no longer wants to pursue a way of life which is so fucked-up, repenting of how he’s treated his girlfriend and that he’s “learned you can’t solve every problem with a bullet”. Then when you just think you’ve seen a character arc, a tiny fraction of character development, pathetic, self-serving and ham-fisted though it might be - BOOM! He grins “I’m just fucking with you” and ends the book with an address to the reader about how pathetic normal life is, how much we should envy him and his big swinging dick lifestyle, and how fucking pathetic we all are for having read about his adventure and envied him and are now going back to our small, shallow, pathetic lives. It ends on the lines “You’re just going to close this book and buy something else to fill that big, empty void we’ve created in your life. This is my face while I’m fucking you in the ass”. Okay, a number of things. You don’t insult your reader for reading your book. Ever. Also, if you have any integrity, any at all, you don’t fill your character’s mouth with lines he would never say, just for the fun of it. As a writer, to have so little commitment to your story as to have your character spout shit just because you think it’d be hilarious to end to the book on a ‘Psyche! Gotcha!’ shows a despicable lack of care about what you’re putting out.<br /><br />So in conclusion, if you think the smartest thing you’ve ever seen is a Rubik’s cube getting solved really fast, this book is for you. If you resent working for your AFRICAN-AMERICAN BOSS, this book is for you. If you think there’s nothing cooler than to be free from society’s rules to go on a raping spree, this book is for you. You’re a moron.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17892104996530557822" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1373044574" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1373044574" />
    <a title="Willow Redd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16423892-willow-redd"><img alt="Willow Redd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365568572p2/16423892.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1373044574?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willow Redd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willow Redd" href="/user/show/16423892-willow-redd">Willow Redd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781582409337" href="/book/show/2568566-wanted">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1373044574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9935919508672078106">Wanted, as well as being a flip of the traditional superhero origin story (telling the origin of the newest supervillain instead), is also a nice commentary on the dark and gritty comics that have grown more and more in popularity since 1985. The whole thing, to use the parlance of the book, is kind of a big "f-u" to the comics industry, from reader to creator. "Because, who reads comics anymore?"<br/><br/>The world of Wanted is one where the supervillains have won. In fact, they won in 1986 and removed a</span>
  <span id="freeText9935919508672078106" style="display:none">Wanted, as well as being a flip of the traditional superhero origin story (telling the origin of the newest supervillain instead), is also a nice commentary on the dark and gritty comics that have grown more and more in popularity since 1985. The whole thing, to use the parlance of the book, is kind of a big "f-u" to the comics industry, from reader to creator. "Because, who reads comics anymore?"<br /><br />The world of Wanted is one where the supervillains have won. In fact, they won in 1986 and removed any knowledge of the defeated heroes from reality, leaving the world a grim and gritty place indeed, the one outside your window. In this world we are introduced to Wesley Gibson, perennial wuss with a life nobody wants, but most suffer through. Until he's told that he's the son and inheritor of one of the baddest supervillains to ever walk the Earth, recently deceased.<br /><br />We see Wesley go from simpering pacifist to stone-cold killer as he takes over his pop's career and learns the ropes in this new world of the Fraternity, the supervillain secret society that has been running the world ever since they defeated the heroes. Of course, like most giant criminal syndicates, there's dissent in the ranks, and Wesley's right in the middle.<br /><br />This particular edition is packed with goodies as well. Along with the Dossier that was part of the series run with artwork from some top comic names, there are also interviews and commentaries on the making-of, as well as a look at some alternate panels that were replaced and a cover gallery.<br /><br />Definitely an enjoyable read because, as I said, it not only presents a grim and gritty look at the comic world, but it also pokes fun at the reasons grim and gritty comic worlds came into being. Also, the final commentary from Wesley is a little thought-provoking, with a final image that will certainly shock and stick with the reader long after they put this one on the shelf.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9935919508672078106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1023449541" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1023449541" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27727871-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1023449541?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/27727871-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1023449541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13842890077097913048">There is something eerily reminiscent in the style of Wanted to the writings of an adolescent school shooter. At one point in the story, the main character brags that while some of the other characters can ride sound waves or walk through walls, he's just good at killing people, and that's fine with him. Swell. From start to finish Wanted is an adolescent power trip for disturbed teenage boys. Anyone else is going to find the graphic novel either dull or silly (at times it is both).<br/><br/>Every page of</span>
  <span id="freeText13842890077097913048" style="display:none">There is something eerily reminiscent in the style of Wanted to the writings of an adolescent school shooter. At one point in the story, the main character brags that while some of the other characters can ride sound waves or walk through walls, he's just good at killing people, and that's fine with him. Swell. From start to finish Wanted is an adolescent power trip for disturbed teenage boys. Anyone else is going to find the graphic novel either dull or silly (at times it is both).<br /><br />Every page of Wanted is pervaded with a palpable feeling that the writer has seen Fight Club a whole bunch of times, and is determined to bring the super-cool underground sensibilities of that mass-market mega-blockbuster to the pages of comics. Fine. Be prepared for a lot of swears, and even more violence. That wouldn't be a deal-breaker if the story were in any way interesting, but it isn't.<br /><br />The plot of Wanted revolves around a loser main character who suddenly finds out that the world is run by super-villains, and he is heir to the throne. Murder, rape, and general mayhem ensue. The idea that super-villains have won their war against heroes is a good one, and begs the question, what next? What do super villains do when they win? Apparently they set up a bureaucracy and then make a lot of money. OK. If that's all they wanted, why didn't they just get jobs in the field of finance? If this sounds boring, then guess what? It is! The author tries to liven things up with random murder and rapes, but the attempts feel increasingly forced.<br /><br />Wanted is a poor, violent, vulgar graphic novel. But worst of all, it is boring. Go read Watchmen or the Sandman books instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13842890077097913048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Anyway, Wesley's life changes and he becomes The Killer, an expert marksm</span>
  <span id="freeText7364229190823805789" style="display:none">It's Eminem and Halle Berry against the world. (Read the book to find out :P)<br /><br />Wesley lives a miserable life, with a cheating girlfriend and the constant rebukes of his boss. But he cannot do anything about it, because he doesn't want to confront them. Then one day he gets swept into the world of supervillains...yes, there's a cabal of supervillains that has divided the world among its five members, kinda like The Godfather. Anyway, Wesley's life changes and he becomes The Killer, an expert marksman (like James McAvoy in the movie minus the projectiles).<br /><br />The story is set in a reality where the bad guys have won and superheroes can be found only in comicbooks. The plot serves as the basis for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6238080.Wolverine_Old_Man_Logan" title="Wolverine Old Man Logan by Mark Millar" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolverine: Old Man Logan</a>, another Millar masterpiece. One can find references to Superman, Batman, Wonder Woman, The Comedian, Catwoman, Lex Luthor, Clayface and many more. It's one of the better Mark Millar books.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vHEk8WJnB3leh8ZEujWkuetklnA4qmXqF8+xTzs18jKT8sLkbcp9wlh/E5tjGrlr2oc8c4asLXczDxk5F6yaQQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LuBJ4bU+JdPcsdIcKvhqmDQ84V5fbbkbK1ioZfQH+UoBY6/0upNfaNpJB8Pz13dKBd9LXeFr8YYPmAAT2J6ROQ==')})
  }

  <span id="freeText961628445033276528" style="display:none">Mark Millar is the New York Times best-selling writer of Wanted, the Kick-Ass series, The Secret Service, Jupiter’s Legacy, Jupiter’s Circle, Nemesis, Superior, Super Crooks, American Jesus, MPH, Starlight, and Chrononauts. Wanted, Kick-Ass, Kick-Ass 2, and The Secret Service (as Kingsman: The Secret Service) have been adapted into feature films, and Nemesis, Superior, Starlight, War Heroes, Jupiter’s Legacy and Chrononauts are in development at major studios. <br /><br />His DC Comics work includes the seminal Superman: Red Son, and at Marvel Comics he created The Ultimates – selected by Time magazine as the comic book of the decade, Wolverine: Old Man Logan, and Civil War – the industry’s biggest-selling superhero series in almost two decades. <br /><br />Mark has been an Executive Producer on all his movie adaptations and is currently creative consultant to Fox Studios on their Marvel slate of movies. <br /><br /><br /></span>
